<p><b>Doris Day</b> (born <b>Doris Mary Ann Kappelhoff</b>; April 3, 1922 or 1924)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discrepancy_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-discrepancy-2">[Note 1]</a></sup> is a retired American actress and singer, and continuing animal welfare activist.</p>
<p>Day began her career as a big band singer in 1939. Her popularity began to rise after her first hit recording "<a href="/wiki/Sentimental_Journey_(song)" title="Sentimental Journey (song)">Sentimental Journey</a>", in 1945. After leaving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Les_Brown_%26_His_Band_of_Renown" title="Les Brown &amp; His Band of Renown">Les Brown &amp; His Band of Renown</a> to embark on a solo career, Day started her long-lasting partnership with <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">Columbia Records</a>, which remained her only recording label. The contract lasted from 1947 to 1967 and included more than 650 recordings, making Day one of the most popular and acclaimed singers of the 20th century. She received the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Lifetime_Achievement_Award" title="Grammy Lifetime Achievement Award">Grammy Lifetime Achievement Award</a> and a Legend Award from the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Singers" title="Society of Singers">Society of Singers</a>. In 2011 – well into her late 80s – she released her 29th studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/My_Heart_(Doris_Day_album)" title="My Heart (Doris Day album)">My Heart</a></i>, which debuted at No. 9 on the UK Top 40 charts, which made her the oldest living artist to score a UK Top 10 with an album featuring new material.</p>
<p>In 1948, Day was persuaded by songwriters <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Cahn" title="Sammy Cahn">Sammy Cahn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jule_Styne" title="Jule Styne">Jule Styne</a> and by Al Levy, her agent at the time, to audition for <i><a href="/wiki/Romance_on_the_High_Seas" title="Romance on the High Seas">Romance on the High Seas</a></i>, which led to a 20-year career in film. She became well known for her string of musicals with <a href="/wiki/Gordon_MacRae" title="Gordon MacRae">Gordon MacRae</a> in the early 1950s, and later, romantic comedies with handsome leading men such as <a href="/wiki/Clark_Gable" title="Clark Gable">Clark Gable</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Teacher%27s_Pet_(1958_film)" title="Teacher's Pet (1958 film)">Teacher's Pet</a></i> (1958), <a href="/wiki/Rock_Hudson" title="Rock Hudson">Rock Hudson</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Pillow_Talk_(film)" title="Pillow Talk (film)">Pillow Talk</a></i> (1959), <i><a href="/wiki/Lover_Come_Back_(1961_film)" title="Lover Come Back (1961 film)">Lover Come Back</a></i> (1961) and <i><a href="/wiki/Send_Me_No_Flowers" title="Send Me No Flowers">Send Me No Flowers</a></i> (1964), <a href="/wiki/Cary_Grant" title="Cary Grant">Cary Grant</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/That_Touch_of_Mink" title="That Touch of Mink">That Touch of Mink</a></i> (1962), and <a href="/wiki/James_Garner" title="James Garner">James Garner</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Thrill_of_It_All" title="The Thrill of It All">The Thrill of It All</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Move_Over,_Darling" title="Move Over, Darling">Move Over, Darling</a></i> (1963). She was ranked the biggest box-office star, the only woman appearing on that list in the era, for four years (1960, 1962, 1963 and 1964), ranking in the top 10 in ten different years (1951–52 and 1959–66). She became the top-ranking female box-office star of all time and is currently ranked sixth among the top 10 box office performers (male and female), as of 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> despite making her last film in 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Day received an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> nomination for her performance in <i>Pillow Talk</i>, won three Henrietta Awards (World Film Favorite), and received the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Film_Critics_Association%27s_Career_Achievement_Award" title="Los Angeles Film Critics Association's Career Achievement Award">Los Angeles Film Critics Association's Career Achievement Award</a>. In 1989, she received the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille_Award" title="Cecil B. DeMille Award">Cecil B. DeMille Award</a> for lifetime achievement in motion pictures. Her strong commitment to animal welfare began in 1971, when she co-founded Actors and Others for Animals. She started her own non-profit organization in the 1970s, the Doris Day Animal Foundation and, later, the <a href="/wiki/Doris_Day_Animal_League" title="Doris Day Animal League">Doris Day Animal League</a> (DDAL). Establishing the annual observance <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spay_Day_USA" title="Spay Day USA">Spay Day USA</a> in 1995, the Doris Day Animal League now partners with <a href="/wiki/The_Humane_Society_of_the_United_States" title="The Humane Society of the United States">The Humane Society of the United States</a> and continues to be a leading advocacy organization. In 2004, she received the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> from President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in recognition of her distinguished service to the country. Day is retired from acting and performing, but has continued her work in animal rights and welfare causes.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Doris Mary Ann Kappelhoff<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> was born on April 3, 1922 or 1924 in <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a>, the daughter of Alma Sophia (née Welz), a housewife, and William Joseph Kappelhoff, a music teacher and choir master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufman20084_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufman20084-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-census-8">[7]</a></sup> All of her grandparents were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_American" title="German American">German immigrants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The youngest of three siblings, she had two older brothers: Richard (who died before her birth) and Paul, several years older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchner197518_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchner197518-10">[9]</a></sup> Due to her father's alleged infidelity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> her parents separated. She developed an early interest in dance, and in the mid-1930s formed a dance duo with Jerry Doherty that performed locally in Cincinnati.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParishPitts2003_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-ParishPitts2003-12">[11]</a></sup> A car accident on October 13, 1937, injured her legs and curtailed her prospects as a professional dancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrowneBrowne2001_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrowneBrowne2001-14">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career_.281938.E2.80.931947.29">Early career (1938–1947)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early career (1938–1947)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doris_Day,_Aquarium,_gottlieb.01841.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4984" data-file-width="3680" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Doris_Day%2C_Aquarium%2C_gottlieb.01841.jpg/170px-Doris_Day%2C_Aquarium%2C_gottlieb.01841.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Doris_Day%2C_Aquarium%2C_gottlieb.01841.jpg/255px-Doris_Day%2C_Aquarium%2C_gottlieb.01841.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Doris_Day%2C_Aquarium%2C_gottlieb.01841.jpg/340px-Doris_Day%2C_Aquarium%2C_gottlieb.01841.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doris_Day,_Aquarium,_gottlieb.01841.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
At the Aquarium Jazz Club, New York (1946)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While recovering, Day started to sing along with the radio and discovered a talent she did not know she had. Day said: "During this long, boring period, I used to while away a lot of time listening to the radio, sometimes singing along with the likes of <a href="/wiki/Benny_Goodman" title="Benny Goodman">Benny Goodman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_Ellington" title="Duke Ellington">Duke Ellington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Dorsey" title="Tommy Dorsey">Tommy Dorsey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Miller" title="Glenn Miller">Glenn Miller</a> [...]. But the one radio voice I listened to above others belonged to <a href="/wiki/Ella_Fitzgerald" title="Ella Fitzgerald">Ella Fitzgerald</a>. There was a quality to her voice that fascinated me, and I'd sing along with her, trying to catch the subtle ways she shaded her voice, the casual yet clean way she sang the words."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchner197538.E2.80.9339_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchner197538.E2.80.9339-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Observing her daughter rekindled Alma's interest in show business, and she decided to give Doris singing lessons. She engaged a teacher, Grace Raine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchner197538_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchner197538-16">[15]</a></sup> After three lessons, Raine told Alma that young Doris had "tremendous potential", which led Alma to give her daughter three lessons a week for the price of one. Years later, Day said that Raine had the biggest effect on her singing style and career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchner197538.E2.80.9339_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchner197538.E2.80.9339-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the eight months she was taking singing lessons, Day had her first professional jobs as a vocalist, on the <a href="/wiki/WLW" title="WLW">WLW</a> radio program <i>Carlin's Carnival</i>, and in a local restaurant, Charlie Yee's Shanghai Inn. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchner197540.E2.80.9341_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchner197540.E2.80.9341-17">[16]</a></sup> During her radio performances, Day first caught the attention of <a href="/wiki/Barney_Rapp" title="Barney Rapp">Barney Rapp</a>, who was looking for a girl vocalist and asked if Day would like to audition for the job. According to Rapp, he had auditioned about 200 singers when Day got the job.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchner197544_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchner197544-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>While working for Rapp in 1939, she adopted the stage surname "Day", at Rapp's suggestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Rapp felt that "Kappelhoff" was too long for <a href="/wiki/Marquee_(sign)" title="Marquee (sign)">marquees</a>, and he admired her rendition of the song "Day After Day".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufman200822_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufman200822-20">[19]</a></sup> After working with Rapp, Day worked with bandleaders Jimmy James,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bob_Crosby" title="Bob Crosby">Bob Crosby</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sutro2011_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sutro2011-22">[21]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Les_Brown_(bandleader)" title="Les Brown (bandleader)">Les Brown</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Family2009_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Family2009-23">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>While working with Brown, Day scored her first hit recording, "<a href="/wiki/Sentimental_Journey_(song)" title="Sentimental Journey (song)">Sentimental Journey</a>", released in early 1945. It soon became an anthem of the desire of World War II demobilizing troops to return home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pc1b_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-pc1b-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santopietro2008_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Santopietro2008-25">[24]</a></sup> This song is still associated with Day, and she rerecorded it on several occasions, including a version in her 1971 television special.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBraun200426_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBraun200426-26">[25]</a></sup> During 1945–46, Day (as vocalist with the Les Brown Band) had six other top ten hits on the <i>Billboard</i> chart: "<a href="/wiki/My_Dreams_Are_Getting_Better_All_the_Time" title="My Dreams Are Getting Better All the Time">My Dreams Are Getting Better All the Time</a>", "'Tain't Me", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Till_the_End_of_Time_(Buddy_Kaye_and_Ted_Mossman_song)" title="Till the End of Time (Buddy Kaye and Ted Mossman song)">Till The End of Time</a>", "You Won't Be Satisfied (Until You Break My Heart)", "The Whole World is Singing My Song", and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I_Got_the_Sun_in_the_Mornin%27" title="I Got the Sun in the Mornin'">I Got the Sun in the Mornin'</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> By the time she left Brown's band in August 1946, she was the highest paid female band vocalist in the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_film_career_.281948.E2.80.931954.29">Early film career (1948–1954)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early film career (1948–1954)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Doris_Day_filmography" title="Doris Day filmography">Doris Day filmography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Starlift_DorisDay_and_GordonMacRae.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="566" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Starlift_DorisDay_and_GordonMacRae.jpg/220px-Starlift_DorisDay_and_GordonMacRae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Starlift_DorisDay_and_GordonMacRae.jpg/330px-Starlift_DorisDay_and_GordonMacRae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Starlift_DorisDay_and_GordonMacRae.jpg/440px-Starlift_DorisDay_and_GordonMacRae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Starlift_DorisDay_and_GordonMacRae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
With <a href="/wiki/Gordon_MacRae" title="Gordon MacRae">Gordon MacRae</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Starlift" title="Starlift">Starlift</a></i> (1951)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calamity_Jane_trailer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="626" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Calamity_Jane_trailer.jpg/220px-Calamity_Jane_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Calamity_Jane_trailer.jpg/330px-Calamity_Jane_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Calamity_Jane_trailer.jpg/440px-Calamity_Jane_trailer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calamity_Jane_trailer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Doris Day and <a href="/wiki/Howard_Keel" title="Howard Keel">Howard Keel</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Calamity_Jane_(film)" title="Calamity Jane (film)">Calamity Jane</a></i> (1953)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While singing with the Les Brown band and for nearly two years on Bob Hope's weekly radio program,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrowneBrowne2001_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrowneBrowne2001-14">[13]</a></sup> she toured extensively across the United States. Her popularity as a radio performer and vocalist, which included a second hit record "<a href="/wiki/My_Dreams_Are_Getting_Better_All_the_Time" title="My Dreams Are Getting Better All the Time">My Dreams Are Getting Better All the Time</a>", led directly to a career in films. In 1941, Day appeared as a singer with the Les Brown band in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soundie" title="Soundie">soundie</a> (a Cinemasters production).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TerenzioMacGillivray1991_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-TerenzioMacGillivray1991-28">[27]</a></sup> During her separation from her second husband, <a href="/wiki/George_William_Weidler" title="George William Weidler">George Weidler</a>, in 1947, Day reportedly intended to leave Los Angeles and return to <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a>. Her agent Al Levy convinced her to attend a party at the home of composer <a href="/wiki/Jule_Styne" title="Jule Styne">Jule Styne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun2007_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braun2007-29">[28]</a></sup> Her performance of the song "<a href="/wiki/Embraceable_You" title="Embraceable You">Embraceable You</a>" impressed Styne and his partner, <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Cahn" title="Sammy Cahn">Sammy Cahn</a>, and they recommended her for a role in <i><a href="/wiki/Romance_on_the_High_Seas" title="Romance on the High Seas">Romance on the High Seas</a></i>, which they were working on for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warner_Brothers" title="Warner Brothers">Warner Brothers</a>. <a href="/wiki/Betty_Hutton" title="Betty Hutton">Betty Hutton</a>'s withdrawal due to pregnancy left the main role to be re-cast, and Day got the part after auditioning for <a href="/wiki/Michael_Curtiz" title="Michael Curtiz">Michael Curtiz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchner197591_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchner197591-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gentry2008_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gentry2008-31">[30]</a></sup> The film provided her with a #2 hit recording as a soloist, "<a href="/wiki/It%27s_Magic" title="It's Magic">It's Magic</a>", which followed by two months her first #1 hit ("<a href="/wiki/Love_Somebody_(1947_song)" title="Love Somebody (1947 song)">Love Somebody</a>" in 1948) recorded as a duet with <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Clark" title="Buddy Clark">Buddy Clark</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Day recorded the song "Someone Like You", written by <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Blane" title="Ralph Blane">Ralph Blane</a> and composed by <a href="/wiki/Harry_Warren" title="Harry Warren">Harry Warren</a>, in circa 1948–49 before the 1949 film <i><a href="/wiki/My_Dream_Is_Yours" title="My Dream Is Yours">My Dream Is Yours</a></i>, which featured the song. The song was given an 81 overall rating with conductor <a href="/wiki/George_Siravo" title="George Siravo">George Siravo</a>'s score praised as "splendid".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> It was subsequently recorded by <a href="/wiki/Ella_Fitzgerald" title="Ella Fitzgerald">Ella Fitzgerald</a> in 1949<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Lee" title="Peggy Lee">Peggy Lee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1950, U.S. servicemen in Korea voted her their favorite star. She continued to make minor and frequently nostalgic period musicals such as <i><a href="/wiki/On_Moonlight_Bay_(film)" title="On Moonlight Bay (film)">On Moonlight Bay</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/By_the_Light_of_the_Silvery_Moon_(film)" title="By the Light of the Silvery Moon (film)">By the Light of the Silvery Moon</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Tea_for_Two_(film)" title="Tea for Two (film)">Tea For Two</a></i> for Warner Brothers. Her most commercially successful film for Warners was <i><a href="/wiki/I%27ll_See_You_in_My_Dreams_(1951_film)" title="I'll See You in My Dreams (1951 film)">I'll See You in My Dreams</a></i>, which broke box-office records of 20 years' standing during its premiere engagement at <a href="/wiki/Radio_City_Music_Hall" title="Radio City Music Hall">Radio City Music Hall</a> in 1951. In 1953, Day appeared as the title character in the comedic western-themed musical, <i><a href="/wiki/Calamity_Jane_(film)" title="Calamity Jane (film)">Calamity Jane</a></i>, winning the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Song" title="Academy Award for Best Original Song">Academy Award for Best Original Song</a> for "<a href="/wiki/Secret_Love_(Doris_Day_song)" title="Secret Love (Doris Day song)">Secret Love</a>" (her recording of which became her fourth #1 hit single in the U.S.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler2008_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler2008-36">[35]</a></sup> Between 1950 and 1953, the albums from six of her movie musicals charted in the Top 10, three of them at #1. After filming <i><a href="/wiki/Lucky_Me_(film)" title="Lucky Me (film)">Lucky Me</a></i> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bob_Cummings" title="Bob Cummings">Bob Cummings</a> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Young_at_Heart_(1954_film)" title="Young at Heart (1954 film)">Young at Heart</a></i> (both 1954) with <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a>, Day chose not to renew her contract with <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Brothers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palmer2010_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer2010-37">[36]</a></sup> She elected to work under the advice and management of her third husband, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marty_Melcher" title="Marty Melcher">Marty Melcher</a>, whom she married in <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank</a> on April 3, 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParishPitts2003_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-ParishPitts2003-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrowneBrowne2001_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-BrowneBrowne2001-14">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breakthrough_.281955.E2.80.931958.29">Breakthrough (1955–1958)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Breakthrough (1955–1958)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doris_Day_in_Love_Me_or_Leave_Me_trailer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="284" data-file-width="400" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Doris_Day_in_Love_Me_or_Leave_Me_trailer.jpg/220px-Doris_Day_in_Love_Me_or_Leave_Me_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Doris_Day_in_Love_Me_or_Leave_Me_trailer.jpg/330px-Doris_Day_in_Love_Me_or_Leave_Me_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a5/Doris_Day_in_Love_Me_or_Leave_Me_trailer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doris_Day_in_Love_Me_or_Leave_Me_trailer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Day in the trailer for <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Me_or_Leave_Me_(film)" title="Love Me or Leave Me (film)">Love Me or Leave Me</a></i> (1955)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Having become primarily recognized as a musical-comedy actress, Day gradually took on more dramatic roles to broaden her range. Her dramatic star-turn as singer <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Etting" title="Ruth Etting">Ruth Etting</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Me_or_Leave_Me_(film)" title="Love Me or Leave Me (film)">Love Me or Leave Me</a></i> (1955), co-starring <a href="/wiki/James_Cagney" title="James Cagney">James Cagney</a>, received critical and commercial success, becoming Day's biggest hit thus far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LisantiPaul2002_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-LisantiPaul2002-38">[37]</a></sup> Day would later call it, in her autobiography, her best film performance. Producer Joe Pasternak said, "I was stunned that Doris didn't get an Oscar nomination."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The soundtrack album from that movie was a No. 1 hit that stayed charted for twenty-eight weeks and became the recording industry's third biggest selling album of the entire decade.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Day starred in <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a>'s suspense film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Knew_Too_Much_(1956_film)" title="The Man Who Knew Too Much (1956 film)">The Man Who Knew Too Much</a></i> (1956) with <a href="/wiki/James_Stewart" title="James Stewart">James Stewart</a>. She sang only two songs in the film, "<a href="/wiki/Que_Sera,_Sera_(Whatever_Will_Be,_Will_Be)" title="Que Sera, Sera (Whatever Will Be, Will Be)">Que Sera, Sera (Whatever Will Be, Will Be)</a>", which won an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Song" title="Academy Award for Best Original Song">Academy Award for Best Original Song</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler2008_2_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler2008_2-39">[38]</a></sup> and "We'll Love Again". During the filming, Day became concerned about Hitchcock's lack of direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falk2007_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Falk2007-40">[39]</a></sup> She recalled being worried if she was pleasing him and confronted him on her performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fawell2001_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fawell2001-41">[40]</a></sup> He told her, "If you weren't doing what I liked, you'd know." At the premiere, Hitchcock was asked how he got such a great performance from Day. He replied, "It wasn't me; it was Doris." The film was Day's 10th movie to be in the Top 10 at the box office. Day played the title role in the thriller/noir <i><a href="/wiki/Julie_(1956_film)" title="Julie (1956 film)">Julie</a></i> (1956) with <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jourdan" title="Louis Jourdan">Louis Jourdan</a>. The film received poor press acclaim and was unpopular with audiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imdb_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-imdb-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>After three successive dramatic films, Day returned to her musical/comedic roots in 1957's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pajama_Game_(film)" title="The Pajama Game (film)">The Pajama Game</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/John_Raitt" title="John Raitt">John Raitt</a>. The film was based on the <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a> play of <a href="/wiki/The_Pajama_Game" title="The Pajama Game">the same name</a>. She worked with <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a> for the comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Teacher%27s_Pet_(1958_film)" title="Teacher's Pet (1958 film)">Teacher's Pet</a></i> (1958), alongside <a href="/wiki/Clark_Gable" title="Clark Gable">Clark Gable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gig_Young" title="Gig Young">Gig Young</a>. She co-starred with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Widmark" title="Richard Widmark">Richard Widmark</a> and Gig Young in the romantic comedy film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tunnel_of_Love" title="The Tunnel of Love">The Tunnel of Love</a></i> (1958), but found scant success opposite <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lemmon" title="Jack Lemmon">Jack Lemmon</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/It_Happened_to_Jane" title="It Happened to Jane">It Happened to Jane</a></i> (1959).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imdb_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-imdb-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Billboard</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> annual nationwide poll of disc jockeys had ranked Day as the No. 1 female vocalist nine times in ten years (1949 through 1958), but her success and popularity as a singer was now being overshadowed by her box-office appeal.{<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>}</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Box-office_success_.281959.E2.80.931968.29">Box-office success (1959–1968)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Box-office success (1959–1968)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Day-midnightlace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="582" data-file-width="480" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Day-midnightlace.jpg/170px-Day-midnightlace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Day-midnightlace.jpg/255px-Day-midnightlace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Day-midnightlace.jpg/340px-Day-midnightlace.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Day-midnightlace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Day in publicity portrait for <i><a href="/wiki/Midnight_Lace" title="Midnight Lace">Midnight Lace</a></i> (1960)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1959, Day entered her most successful phase as a film actress with a series of romantic comedies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gourley2008_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gourley2008-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monteith2008_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monteith2008-45">[44]</a></sup> This success began with <i><a href="/wiki/Pillow_Talk_(film)" title="Pillow Talk (film)">Pillow Talk</a></i> (1959), co-starring <a href="/wiki/Rock_Hudson" title="Rock Hudson">Rock Hudson</a>, who became a lifelong friend, and <a href="/wiki/Tony_Randall" title="Tony Randall">Tony Randall</a>. Day received a nomination for an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actress" title="Academy Award for Best Actress">Academy Award for Best Actress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finler2003_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Finler2003-46">[45]</a></sup> Day, Hudson, and Randall made two more films together, <i><a href="/wiki/Lover_Come_Back_(1961_film)" title="Lover Come Back (1961 film)">Lover Come Back</a></i> (1961) and <i><a href="/wiki/Send_Me_No_Flowers" title="Send Me No Flowers">Send Me No Flowers</a></i> (1964).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glitre2006_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glitre2006-47">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>She also starred with <a href="/wiki/David_Niven" title="David Niven">David Niven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Janis_Paige" title="Janis Paige">Janis Paige</a> in the hit <i><a href="/wiki/Please_Don%27t_Eat_the_Daisies_(film)" title="Please Don't Eat the Daisies (film)">Please Don't Eat the Daisies</a></i>. In 1962, Day appeared with Cary Grant in the comedy <i><a href="/wiki/That_Touch_of_Mink" title="That Touch of Mink">That Touch of Mink</a></i>, the first film in history ever to gross $1 million in one theatre (Radio City Music Hall). Day was in the Top 10 at the box office ten times. During 1960 and the 1962 to 1964 period, she ranked number one at the box office, the second woman to be number one four times. She set an unprecedented record that has yet to be equaled, receiving seven consecutive <a href="/wiki/Laurel_Awards" title="Laurel Awards">Laurel Awards</a> as the top female box office star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morris1976_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morris1976-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Day teamed up with <a href="/wiki/James_Garner" title="James Garner">James Garner</a>, starting with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Thrill_of_It_All" title="The Thrill of It All">The Thrill of It All</a></i>, followed by <i><a href="/wiki/Move_Over,_Darling" title="Move Over, Darling">Move Over, Darling</a></i> (both 1963). <i>Move Over, Darling</i> was originally titled <i><a href="/wiki/Something%27s_Got_to_Give" title="Something's Got to Give">Something's Got to Give</a></i>, a 1962 comeback vehicle for <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Monroe" title="Marilyn Monroe">Marilyn Monroe</a>. Filming was suspended following Monroe's dismissal and her subsequent death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamrick2004_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamrick2004-49">[48]</a></sup> A year later, filming resumed with Day recast as the leading lady.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harding2012_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harding2012-50">[49]</a></sup> The film's theme song, "<a href="/wiki/Move_Over_Darling_(song)" title="Move Over Darling (song)">Move Over Darling</a>", co-written by her son, reached #8 in the U.K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilchak2005_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pilchak2005-51">[50]</a></sup> In between these comedic roles, Day co-starred with <a href="/wiki/Rex_Harrison" title="Rex Harrison">Rex Harrison</a> in the movie thriller <i><a href="/wiki/Midnight_Lace" title="Midnight Lace">Midnight Lace</a></i> (1960), an updating of the classic stage thriller, <i><a href="/wiki/Gas_Light" title="Gas Light">Gaslight</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waller1987_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waller1987-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the late 1960s, the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_revolution" title="Sexual revolution">sexual revolution</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baby_boomer" title="Baby boomer">baby boomer</a> generation had refocused public attitudes about sex. Times changed, but Day's films did not. Day's next film, <i><a href="/wiki/Do_Not_Disturb_(1965_film)" title="Do Not Disturb (1965 film)">Do Not Disturb</a></i> (1965), was popular with audiences, but her popularity soon waned. Critics and comics dubbed Day "The World's Oldest Virgin",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> and audiences began to shy away from her films. As a result, she slipped from the list of top box-office stars, last appearing in the top ten in 1966 with the hit film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Glass_Bottom_Boat" title="The Glass Bottom Boat">The Glass Bottom Boat</a></i>. One of the roles she turned down was that of "Mrs. Robinson" in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Graduate" title="The Graduate">The Graduate</a></i>, a role that eventually went to <a href="/wiki/Anne_Bancroft" title="Anne Bancroft">Anne Bancroft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grindon2011_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grindon2011-55">[54]</a></sup> In her published memoirs, Day said she had rejected the part on moral grounds: she found the script "vulgar and offensive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>She starred in the western film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ballad_of_Josie" title="The Ballad of Josie">The Ballad of Josie</a></i> (1967). That same year, Day recorded <i><a href="/wiki/The_Love_Album_(Doris_Day_album)" title="The Love Album (Doris Day album)">The Love Album</a></i>, although it was not released until 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day-57">[56]</a></sup> The following year (1968), she starred in the comedy film <i><a href="/wiki/Where_Were_You_When_the_Lights_Went_Out%3F" title="Where Were You When the Lights Went Out?">Where Were You When the Lights Went Out?</a></i> which centers on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northeast_blackout_of_November_9,_1965" title="Northeast blackout of November 9, 1965">Northeast blackout of November 9, 1965</a>. Her final feature, the comedy <i><a href="/wiki/With_Six_You_Get_Eggroll" title="With Six You Get Eggroll">With Six You Get Eggroll</a></i>, was released in 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCM2013_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-TCM2013-58">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1959-70, Day received nine Laurel Award nominations (and won four times) for best female performance in eight comedies and one drama. From 1959 through 1969, she received six <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Globe" title="Golden Globe">Golden Globe</a> nominations for best female performance in three comedies, one drama (<i>Midnight Lace</i>), one musical (<i>Jumbo</i>), and her television series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bankruptcy_and_television_career">Bankruptcy and television career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Bankruptcy and television career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When her third husband <a href="/wiki/Martin_Melcher" title="Martin Melcher">Martin Melcher</a> died on April 20, 1968, a shocked Day discovered that Melcher and his business partner Jerome Bernard Rosenthal had squandered her earnings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[Note 2]</a></sup> leaving her deeply in debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sonneborn2002_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sonneborn2002-61">[59]</a></sup> Rosenthal had been her attorney since 1949, when he represented her in her uncontested divorce action against her second husband, saxophonist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_W._Weidler" title="George W. Weidler">George W. Weidler</a>. Day filed suit against Rosenthal in February 1969, won a successful decision in 1974, but did not receive compensation until a settlement in 1979 (see details in "1970s", below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metnews_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-metnews-62">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Day also learned that Melcher had committed her to a television series, which became <i><a href="/wiki/The_Doris_Day_Show" title="The Doris Day Show">The Doris Day Show</a></i>.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"It was awful", Day told <i><a href="/wiki/OK!" title="OK!">OK! Magazine</a></i> in 1996. "I was really, really not very well when Marty [Melcher] passed away, and the thought of going into TV was overpowering. But he'd signed me up for a series. And then my son Terry [Melcher] took me walking in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills" title="Beverly Hills">Beverly Hills</a> and explained that it wasn't nearly the end of it. I had also been signed up for a bunch of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TV_specials" title="TV specials">TV specials</a>, all without anyone ever asking me."</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Day hated the idea of performing on television, but felt obliged to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCM2013_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-TCM2013-58">[57]</a></sup> "There was a contract. I didn't know about it. I never wanted to do TV, but I gave it 100 percent anyway. That's the only way I know how to do it."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The first episode of <i>The Doris Day Show</i> aired on September 24, 1968,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> and, from 1968 to 1973, employed "Que Sera, Sera" as its theme song. Day grudgingly persevered (she needed the work to help pay off her debts), but only after <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> ceded creative control to her and her son. The successful show enjoyed a five-year run,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABCSnares_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABCSnares-64">[62]</a></sup> and functioned as a curtain-raiser for the popular <i><a href="/wiki/The_Carol_Burnett_Show" title="The Carol Burnett Show">Carol Burnett Show</a></i>. It is remembered today for its abrupt season-to-season changes in casting and premise. It was not widely syndicated as many of its contemporaries were, and was re-broadcast very little outside the United States, Australia and the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGee2005227.E2.80.9328_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGee2005227.E2.80.9328-65">[63]</a></sup> By the end of its run in 1973, public tastes had changed and her firmly established persona was regarded as passé. She largely retired from acting after <i>The Doris Day Show</i>, but did complete two television specials, <i>The Doris Mary Anne Kappelhoff Special</i> (1971) and <i>Doris Day to Day</i> (1975). She appeared in a <a href="/wiki/John_Denver" title="John Denver">John Denver</a> TV special in 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Day-57">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1985–86 season, Day hosted her own television talk show, <i>Doris Day's Best Friends</i>, on <a href="/wiki/Christian_Broadcasting_Network" title="Christian Broadcasting Network">CBN</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABCSnares_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-ABCSnares-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> The network canceled the show after 26 episodes, despite the worldwide publicity it received.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On September 18, 1974, courts awarded Day $22,835,646 for fraud and <a href="/wiki/Malpractice" title="Malpractice">malpractice</a> in an hour-long oral decision by Superior Judge Lester E. Olson, ending a 99-day trial that involved 18 consolidated lawsuits and countersuits filed by Day and Rosenthal that involved Rosenthal's handling of her finances after she terminated him in July 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metnews_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-metnews-62">[60]</a></sup> The civil trial included 14,451 pages of transcript from 67 witnesses. Represented by attorney Robert Winslow and the law firm of Mitchell, Silberberg &amp; Knupp LLP, Day was awarded $1 million punitive damages, $5.6 million plus $2 million interest for losses incurred in a sham oil venture; $3.4 million plus $1.2 million interest over a hotel venture; $2.2 million plus $793,800 interest for duplicate or unnecessary fees paid to Rosenthal; more than $2 million to recoup loans to Rosenthal; $3.9 million plus $1 million interest for fraud, and $850,000 attorney fees for Day. Olson enjoined Rosenthal from filing any further lawsuits against Day or her business operations. (Rosenthal had filed more than 20 suits from 1969 to 1974). Olson, an expert in complex financial marital settlements, read every page of 3,275 individual exhibits and 68 boxes of miscellaneous financial records. This was the then-largest civil judgment in the state of California. Rosenthal's liability insurer was still able to bring suit against the judgment, finally settling, In October 1979, with Day for about $6 million payable in 23 annual installments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Disbarred1987_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Disbarred1987-67">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rosenthal filed an appeal in the 2nd District Court of Appeal. He filed six additional lawsuits related to the case. Two were libel suits, one against Day and her publishers over comments she made about Rosenthal in her book in which he sought damages. The others sought court determinations that insurance companies and individual lawyers failed to defend Rosenthal properly before Olson and in appellate stages. In April 1979, he filed an unsuccessful suit to set aside the $6 million settlement with Day and recover damages from everyone involved in agreeing, supposedly without his permission, to the payment.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s_and_1990s">1980s and 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1980s and 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 1985, the California Supreme Court rejected Rosenthal's appeal of the multimillion-dollar judgment against him for legal malpractice, and upheld conclusions of a trial court and a Court of Appeal that Rosenthal acted improperly. In April 1986, the U.S. Supreme Court refused to review the lower court's judgment. In June 1987, Rosenthal filed a $30 million lawsuit against lawyers he claimed cheated him out of millions of dollars in real estate investments. He named Day as a co-defendant, describing her as an "unwilling, involuntary plaintiff whose consent cannot be obtained". Rosenthal claimed that millions of dollars Day lost were in real estate sold after Melcher died in 1968, in which Rosenthal asserted that the attorneys gave Day bad advice, telling her to sell, at a loss, three hotels, in <a href="/wiki/Palo_Alto,_California" title="Palo Alto, California">Palo Alto, California</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dallas,_Texas" title="Dallas, Texas">Dallas, Texas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlanta,_Georgia" title="Atlanta, Georgia">Atlanta, Georgia</a> and some oil leases in <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>. Rosenthal claimed he had made the investments under a long-term plan, and did not intend to sell them until they appreciated in value. Two of the hotels sold in 1970 for about $7 million, and their estimated worth in 1986 was $50 million. In July 1984, after a hearing panel of the State Bar Court, after 80 days of testimony and consideration of documentary evidence, the panel accused Rosenthal of 13 separate acts of misconduct and urged his disbarment in a 34-page unsigned opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Disbarred1987_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Disbarred1987-67">[65]</a></sup> The State Bar Court's review department upheld the panel's findings, which asked the justices to order Rosenthal's disbarment. He continued representing clients in federal courts until the U.S. Supreme Court ruled against him on March 21, 1988. Disbarment by the Ninth U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals followed on August 19, 1988. The Supreme Court of California, in affirming the disbarment, held that Rosenthal had engaged in transactions involving undisclosed conflicts of interest, took positions adverse to his former clients, overstated expenses, double-billed for legal fees, failed to return client files, failed to provide access to records, failed to give adequate legal advice, failed to provide clients with an opportunity to obtain independent counsel, filed fraudulent claims, gave false testimony, engaged in conduct designed to harass his clients, delayed court proceedings, obstructed justice and abused legal process. Rosenthal died August 15, 2007, at the age of 96.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Terry_Melcher" title="Terry Melcher">Terry Melcher</a> stated that his adoptive father's premature death saved Day from financial ruin. It remains unresolved whether Marty Melcher had himself also been duped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Champlin1988_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Champlin1988-69">[67]</a></sup> Day stated publicly that she believed her husband innocent of any deliberate wrongdoing, stating that he "simply trusted the wrong person".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Day's autobiography, as told to <a href="/wiki/A._E._Hotchner" title="A. E. Hotchner">A. E. Hotchner</a>, the usually athletic and healthy Martin Melcher had an <a href="/wiki/Cardiomegaly" title="Cardiomegaly">enlarged heart</a>. Most of the interviews on the subject given to Hotchner (and included in Day's autobiography) paint an unflattering portrait of Melcher. Author David Kaufman asserts that one of Day's costars, actor <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jourdan" title="Louis Jourdan">Louis Jourdan</a>, maintained that Day herself disliked her husband,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> but Day's public statements regarding Melcher appear to contradict that assertion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchner1975226_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchner1975226-72">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Day was scheduled to present, along with <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Swayze" title="Patrick Swayze">Patrick Swayze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Hamlisch" title="Marvin Hamlisch">Marvin Hamlisch</a>, the Best Original Score Oscar at the 61st Annual Academy Awards (March 1989) but she suffered a deep leg cut and was unable to attend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> She had been walking through the gardens of her hotel when she cut her leg on a sprinkler. The cut required stitches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Day was inducted into the Ohio Women's Hall of Fame in 1981 and received the Cecil B. DeMille Award for career achievement in 1989. In 1994, Day's Greatest Hits album became another entry into the British charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Day-57">[56]</a></sup> The song "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perhaps,_Perhaps,_Perhaps" title="Perhaps, Perhaps, Perhaps">Perhaps, Perhaps, Perhaps</a>" was included in the soundtrack of the Australian film <i><a href="/wiki/Strictly_Ballroom" title="Strictly Ballroom">Strictly Ballroom</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> the theme song for the British TV show <i><a href="/wiki/Coupling_(UK_TV_series)" title="Coupling (UK TV series)">Coupling</a></i>, with <a href="/wiki/Mari_Wilson" title="Mari Wilson">Mari Wilson</a> performing the song for the title sequence<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> and in the promo for the fourth season of the FX series <i><a href="/wiki/Louie_(TV_series)" title="Louie (TV series)">Louie</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2000, Day received the Ohio Medal of Honor, that state's highest civilian award. In 2006, Day recorded a commentary for the DVD release of the fifth (and final) season of her television show. Day has participated in telephone interviews with a radio station that celebrates her birthday with an annual Doris Day music marathon. In July 2008, she appeared on the <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">Southern California</a> radio show of longtime friend, newscaster <a href="/wiki/George_Putnam_(newsman)" title="George Putnam (newsman)">George Putnam</a>, as reported in the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Day turned down a tribute offer from the <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a> and from the Kennedy Center Honors because they require attendance in person. In 2004, she was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> by President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> for her achievements in the entertainment industry and for her work on behalf of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> President Bush stated, "It was a good day for our fellow creatures when she gave her good heart to the cause of animal welfare."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> Day declined to attend the ceremony due to her <a href="/wiki/Fear_of_flying" title="Fear of flying">fear of flying</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both columnist <a href="/wiki/Liz_Smith_(journalist)" title="Liz Smith (journalist)">Liz Smith</a> and film critic <a href="/wiki/Rex_Reed" title="Rex Reed">Rex Reed</a> have mounted vigorous campaigns to gather support for an honorary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> for Day to herald her film career and her status as the top female box-office star of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> Day received a <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy</a> for Lifetime Achievement in Music in 2008, albeit again in absentia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>She received three Grammy Hall of Fame Awards, in 1998, 1999 and 2012 for her recordings of "Sentimental Journey", "Secret Love", and "Que Sera, Sera", respectively. Day was inducted into the Hit Parade Hall of Fame in 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> and in 2010 received the first Legend Award ever presented by the Society of Singers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-Day-57">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">2010s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 2010s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Day, aged 89, released <i><a href="/wiki/My_Heart_(Doris_Day_album)" title="My Heart (Doris Day album)">My Heart</a></i> in the United Kingdom on September 5, 2011, her first new album in nearly two decades, since the release of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Love_Album_(Doris_Day_album)" title="The Love Album (Doris Day album)">The Love Album</a></i>, which, although recorded in 1967, was not released until 1994. The album is a compilation of previously unreleased recordings produced by Day's son, Terry Melcher, before his death in 2004. Tracks include the 1970s <a href="/wiki/Joe_Cocker" title="Joe Cocker">Joe Cocker</a> hit "<a href="/wiki/You_Are_So_Beautiful" title="You Are So Beautiful">You Are So Beautiful</a>", <a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">The Beach Boys</a>' "<a href="/wiki/Disney_Girls_(1957)" title="Disney Girls (1957)">Disney Girls</a>" and jazz standards such as "<a href="/wiki/My_Buddy_(song)" title="My Buddy (song)">My Buddy</a>", which Day originally sang in her 1951 film <i>I'll See You in My Dreams</i>.</p>
<p>Day has dedicated this song to her son. The disc was released in the US via City Hall Records on December 6, and within two weeks had climbed to No. 12 on Amazon's bestseller list in spite of being priced over 25% higher than most CDs to raise funds for the <a href="/wiki/Doris_Day_Animal_League" title="Doris Day Animal League">Doris Day Animal League</a>. It debuted at 135 on the Billboard 200, Day's first entry on that chart since <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Him_(album)" title="Love Him (album)">Love Him</a></i> (1963).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> Day became the oldest artist to score a UK Top 10 with an album featuring new material, according to the Official Charts Company, entering at No. 9. (British singer <a href="/wiki/Vera_Lynn" title="Vera Lynn">Vera Lynn</a> reached the top of the chart in August 2009 at age 92, but with a greatest hits album, <i>We'll Meet Again – The Very Best of Vera Lynn</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup>)</p>
<p>In January 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Film_Critics_Association" title="Los Angeles Film Critics Association">Los Angeles Film Critics Association</a> presented Day with a Lifetime Achievement Award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since her retirement from films, Day has lived in <a href="/wiki/Carmel-by-the-Sea,_California" title="Carmel-by-the-Sea, California">Carmel-by-the-Sea, California</a>. She has many pets and adopts stray animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Day is a lifelong <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufman2008437_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufman2008437-87">[85]</a></sup> and supported George W. Bush's presidential campaign in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2000" title="United States presidential election, 2000">2000</a>. Her only child, music producer and songwriter <a href="/wiki/Terry_Melcher" title="Terry Melcher">Terry Melcher</a>, who had a hit in the 1960s with "<a href="/wiki/Hey_Little_Cobra" title="Hey Little Cobra">Hey Little Cobra</a>" under the name <a href="/wiki/The_Rip_Chords" title="The Rip Chords">The Rip Chords</a>, died of melanoma in 2004, about five months after Day had received the Presidential Medal of Freedom. She owns a hotel in Carmel-by-the-Sea, the Cypress Inn, which Melcher had co-owned with Day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriages">Marriages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Marriages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1975, Day published her autobiography, <i>Doris Day: Her Own Story</i>, an "as-told-to" work with <a href="/wiki/A._E._Hotchner" title="A. E. Hotchner">A. E. Hotchner</a>. The book detailed her first three marriages:</p>
<ul>
<li>To Al Jorden, a trombonist whom she first met in Barney Rapp's Band, from March 1941 to 1943. Her only child, son <a href="/wiki/Terry_Melcher" title="Terry Melcher">Terrence Paul Jorden (later known as Terry Melcher)</a>, resulted from this marriage. Husband Jorden, who was reportedly physically abusive to Day, committed suicide in 1967 by gunshot.</li>
<li>To <a href="/wiki/George_William_Weidler" title="George William Weidler">George Weidler</a> (a saxophonist), from March 30, 1946 to May 31, 1949. Weidler, the brother of actress <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Weidler" title="Virginia Weidler">Virginia Weidler</a>, and Day met again several years later. During a brief reconciliation, he helped introduce her to Christian Science.</li>
<li>To <a href="/wiki/Martin_Melcher" title="Martin Melcher">Martin Melcher</a>, whom she married on April 3, 1951. This marriage lasted until Melcher's death in 1968. Melcher adopted Day's son Terry, who, with the name <a href="/wiki/Terry_Melcher" title="Terry Melcher">Terry Melcher</a>, became a successful musician and record producer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> Martin Melcher produced many of Day's movies. She and Melcher were both practicing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Scientist" title="Christian Scientist">Christian Scientists</a>, resulting in her not seeing a doctor for some time after symptoms that suggested cancer. This distressing period ended when, finally consulting a physician, and thereby finding the lump was benign, she fully recovered.</li>
</ul>
<p>After publishing her autobiography, Day married one last time:</p>
<ul>
<li>Her fourth marriage, from April 14, 1976 until 1981, was to Barry Comden (March 30, 1935 – May 25, 2009),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ComdenDeath_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ComdenDeath-90">[88]</a></sup> who was roughly a decade younger. Comden was the <i><a href="/wiki/Ma%C3%AEtre_d%27h%C3%B4tel" title="Maître d'hôtel">maître d'hôtel</a></i> at one of Day's favorite restaurants. Knowing of her great love of dogs, Comden endeared himself to Day by giving her a bag of meat scraps and bones on her way out of the restaurant. When this marriage unraveled, Comden complained that Day cared more for her "animal friends" than she did for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ComdenDeath_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-ComdenDeath-90">[88]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_welfare_activism">Animal welfare activism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Animal welfare activism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Day's interest in animal welfare and related issues apparently dates to her teen years. While recovering from an automobile accident, she took her dog Tiny for a walk without a leash. Tiny ran into the street and was killed by a passing car. Day later confessed guilt and loneliness about Tiny's untimely death. In 1971, she co-founded Actors and Others for Animals, and appeared in a series of newspaper advertisements denouncing the wearing of fur, alongside <a href="/wiki/Mary_Tyler_Moore" title="Mary Tyler Moore">Mary Tyler Moore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angie_Dickinson" title="Angie Dickinson">Angie Dickinson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jayne_Meadows" title="Jayne Meadows">Jayne Meadows</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatrickMcGee2006132_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatrickMcGee2006132-91">[89]</a></sup> Day's friend, <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Amory" title="Cleveland Amory">Cleveland Amory</a>, wrote about these events in <i>Man Kind? Our Incredible War on Wildlife</i> (1974).</p>
<p>In 1978, Day founded the Doris Day Pet Foundation, now the Doris Day Animal Foundation (DDAF).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grudens2001_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grudens2001-92">[90]</a></sup> A non-profit 501(c)(3) grant-giving public charity, DDAF funds other non-profit causes throughout the US that share DDAF's mission of helping animals and the people who love them. The DDAF continues to operate independently under Day's personal supervision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>To complement the Doris Day Animal Foundation, Day formed the <a href="/wiki/Doris_Day_Animal_League" title="Doris Day Animal League">Doris Day Animal League</a> (DDAL) in 1987, a national non-profit citizen's lobbying organization whose mission is to reduce pain and suffering and protect animals through legislative initiatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> Day actively lobbied the United States Congress in support of legislation designed to safeguard <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> on a number of occasions and in 1995 she originated the annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spay_Day_USA" title="Spay Day USA">Spay Day USA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> The DDAL merged into <a href="/wiki/The_Humane_Society_of_the_United_States" title="The Humane Society of the United States">The Humane Society of the United States</a> (HSUS) in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> Staff members of DDAL took positions within The HSUS, and Day recorded public service announcements for the organization.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The HSUS now manages World Spay Day, the annual one-day spay/neuter event that Day originated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>A facility to help abused and neglected horses opened in 2011 and bears her name—the Doris Day Horse Rescue and Adoption Center, located in <a href="/wiki/Murchison,_Texas" title="Murchison, Texas">Murchison, Texas</a>, on the grounds of an animal sanctuary started by her late friend, author <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Amory" title="Cleveland Amory">Cleveland Amory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick-Goudreau2011_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick-Goudreau2011-98">[96]</a></sup> Day contributed $250,000 towards the founding of the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discography">Discography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Discography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doris_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-discrepancy-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-discrepancy_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-discrepancy_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">There is a longstanding dispute over whether Day was born in 1922 or 1924. She has given the latter year during her career and elsewhere; but census records and her biographer (David Kaufman) cite the earlier year.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jerome Bernard Rosenthal (born April 1, 1911 – died August 15, 2007) was <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_the_bar_in_the_United_States" title="Admission to the bar in the United States">admitted to the State Bar of California</a> on June 11, 1946 after graduating from the University of Chicago Law School. His clients included Ross Hunter, Van Johnson and Gordon MacRae. Rosenthal was Day's lawyer, business manager, and tax adviser under a May 1956 agreement (in which he was to receive 10% of virtually everything owned or earned by Day and Melcher).</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
